Title: Joint Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management via Answer Set Programming

Published Time: Tue, 23 Jun 2026 03:42:37 GMT

Number of Pages: 96

# Joint Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management via Answer Set Programming 

Alexander Beiser 1[0009 −0009 −4252 −1043] , Markus Hecher 2[0000 −0003 −0131 −6771] ,Nysret Musliu 1[0000 −0002 −3992 −8637] , and Stefan Woltran 1[0000 −0003 −1594 −8972]  

> 1

TU Wien, Vienna, Austria, {alexander.beiser}@tuwien.ac.at  

> 2

University of Potsdam, Germany & CNRS, France 

Abstract. Operational Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management (AT-FCM) balances flight demand with available sector capacity, to ensure safe and efficient operations. Mathematical models enhance operational AT-FCM performance by framing demand-capacity balancing as an optimiza-tion problem, maximizing efficiency while adhering to safety constraints. However, SOTA research optimizes the aircraft trajectories (called ATFM) or the sector configuration (called DAC) separately. This leaves a research gap of whether joint optimization of ATFM and DAC can bring benefits. We partially address this limitation by introducing a joint ATFCM model with an encoding in Answer Set Programming (ASP). The ASP imple-mentation is evaluated against two baselines applied to our joint model: a SOTA Mixed Integer Programming (MIP) model and an iterative CASA-based heuristic. Computational experiments utilize an instance generator fitted to historical OpenSky Network flight data. Our results indicate that the ASP model outperforms the MIP model, while ASP remains competitive against heuristics on small instances. Furthermore, while DAC has the largest improvement on solving performance compared to rerouting and delaying, unrestricted variants of DAC or rerouting lead to search space thrashing. 

Keywords: Answer Set Programming · Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management · Dynamic Airspace Configuration · Joint Optimization. 

## 1 Introduction 

Operational Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management (ATFCM) is essential to maintaining safe and efficient air traffic. ATFCM aims to balance demand and capacity. Demand can be understood as the number of flights that wish to operate in a sector at a specific time, while capacity is the maximum number of flights a sector can handle in a time window [ 9]. Demand must be kept below capacity, as overloading a sector increases the risk of safety incidents. This is achieved by delaying or rerouting aircraft, or restructuring airspace. However, at the time of writing, ATFCM faces unprecedented challenges due to multiple factors, including air traffic controller shortages and increasing traffic demand [ 21 ]. These challenges lead to an increase in demand, with a  

> arXiv:2606.22978v1 [cs.AI] 22 Jun 2026 2Beiser et. al.

simultaneous decrease in capacity. Tackling this imbalance is non-trivial; however, scientific endeavors are making progress on mathematical models suitable for optimization. Existing ATFCM research can be split into work that focuses on increasing the efficiency of Air Traffic Flow Management (ATFM) and optimizing airspace via Dynamic Airspace Configuration (DAC). ATFM focuses on measures to keep demand below capacity by delaying aircraft or rerouting them. Their models assume a static or pre-determined airspace and methodologically often use Mixed Integer (linear) Programming (MIP) [ 3, 11 , 13 ]. In contrast to this, DAC research uses methods based on geometric, graph-based, or trajectory-based approaches [18], but keeps flight plans fixed. Nevertheless, gaps remain. First, joint optimization of ATFM and DAC can potentially yield performance improvements, due to higher degrees of freedom. Second, the dominance of MIP raises the question whether declarative logic programming methods such as Answer Set Programming (ASP) [ 14 ] can serve as a viable formalization language. From an ASP perspective, joint ATFCM is an interesting real-world setting with interacting hard and soft constraints (safety, efficiency, and fairness/stability). 

Contributions . We address these gaps by proposing a novel ATFCM model: 

– We present the first combined air traffic flow and capacity management model for simultaneous optimization in ASP. 

– We provide an instance generator for small instances fitted to open flight data (including the OpenSky Network COVID-19 dataset), enabling controlled scaling studies across geographic regions. 

– We present the first ATFCM ASP encoding, which we benchmark on data sampled from the instance generator. We compare the ASP encoding to simulated state-of-the-art (SOTA) MIP and heuristic algorithms, and finally perform an ablation study to investigate the impact of enabling / partially disabling/disabling actions. Our findings indicate (1) promising results of ASP, (2) that restricting the search spaces of DAC and rerouting improves results under a fixed compute budget, and (3) that computational bottlenecks in both solving and grounding remain for unbounded variants. After the introduction (Section 1) and the preliminaries (Section 2), we present the ATFCM model with the ASP encoding in Section 3. Section 4 shows the data pipeline, Section 5 the experiments, and Section 6 our conclusions. 

## 2 Preliminaries and Related Work 

Answer Set Programming . ASP is a logic programming paradigm based on non-monotonic reasoning [ 14 ]. A program is a set of rules p1(X1) ∨ . . . ∨ pℓ(Xℓ) ←

pℓ+1 (Xℓ+1 ), . . . , p m(Xm), ¬pm+1 (Xm+1 ), . . . , ¬pn(Xn), where pi(Xi) is a literal, 

l, m, n are non-negative integers s.t. l ≤ m ≤ n, and Xi = ⟨x1, . . . , x z ⟩ is a term vector comprising constants and variables. Grounding refers to the instantiation of variables by all possible domain values, resulting in a ground program. Semantics of ground programs is defined via Gelfond-Lifschitz reduct . Weak constraints 

enable optimization [ 5]. ASP is attractive for ATFCM because it supports rapid modeling of constraints, and lexicographic optimization. Joint ATFCM via ASP 3

Setting. Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management (ATFCM) is the layer of Air Traffic Management that aims to keep demand below capacity [ 9]. Capacity is primarily driven by controller workload: airspace is partitioned into sectors 

and each sector admits at most X flights per hour. For filed flights (requested/-planned operations), a network manager considers interventions that trade off safety (no overload) and efficiency (delay, fuel, distance, stability). In practice, common levers are (i) ground delay, (ii) rerouting within (constrained) acceptable trajectories, and (iii) Dynamic Airspace Configuration (DAC). While (i) and (ii) operate on a per-aircraft trajectory basis, (iii) operates on a sector level. 

Related work . In European operations, ATFM departure regulations are largely computed by the Computer Assisted Slot Allocation (CASA) algorithm, which fol-lows a “First-Planned, First-Served” principle [ 6]. CASA is known to be efficiency-suboptimal; even early mathematical optimization models improved total delay and were later extended with additional criteria (e.g., fairness) [ 23 ]. Bertsimas and Patterson showed that the ATFM model for static airspaces and static routes, but dynamic delaying, is already NP -hard [ 3]. On the DAC side, many approaches construct sectorizations in continuous airspace using heuristics (e.g., genetic algorithms, Voronoi diagrams) [ 24 ] or exploit graph/flow and learning-based methods [ 8,7 ]. Model-wise, our approach occupies a middle ground between bottom-up sector construction by combining atomic sector structures and airspace partitioning. From a logic programming perspective, the aviation literature con-tains only isolated uses (e.g., drones/vertiports) [ 19 , 16 ]. Still, ASP is used in many other industrial domains [ 1, 12 , 2]. We provide the first ASP encoding for joint ATFCM, compare it to SOTA methods, conduct an ablation study, and investigate whether ATFCM suffers from the grounding bottleneck. 

## 3 Joint ATFCM Model via ASP 

In this section, we present the basics of our ATFCM model by following a running example based on Figure 1b. Our model is based on the basic structure of the 

navpoint graph . Aircraft fly between navpoints or airports along airways on the navpoint graph. We assume that flights can not take other maneuvers and must stay on the airways. With this, we can define our navpoint graph structure. 

Definition 1 (Navpoint Graph) The navpoint graph G = ( V, E ) is undirected and labeled. Each v ∈ V is either a navpoint or an airport and has an associated coordinate (lat, lon, alt ) ∈ R3. Edges (airways) e = ( vi, v j ) ∈ E connect vertices. Distances of airways are given by the geodesic distance d : V 2 → R.Example 1. G is defined according to Figure 1b. v0 = (50 , 14 , 100) , v1 = (48 , 14 , 100) , v2 = (46 , 14 , 100) . and Va = {a0, a 1} with 

a0 = (48 .3536 , 11 .7832 , 0) and a1 = (48 .1179 , 16 .5566 , 0) , so V = V ′ ∪ Va, and further, E = {(a0, v 0), (a0, v 1), (a0, v 2), (v0, a 1), (v1, a 1), (v2, a 1)}. For the sake of this running example, we approximate the distances to be d(a0, v 0) = d(a0, v 2) = 

d(a1, v 0) = d(a1, v 2) = 290[ km ] and d(a0, v 1) = d(a1, v 1) = 180[ km ].4 Beiser et. al. Sector

Definition 2 (Time Model) Time is discretized into timesteps ti ∈ T , with 

Tgran timesteps per hour where Tgran divides 60 such that the timestep length is defined in minutes by Ts = 60  

> Tgran

. The set of timesteps T , is for dur ∈ N given by 

T = {t | t ∈ N, 1 ≤ t ≤ dur · Tgran } ∪ { 0}.Example 2. We consider Tgran = 1 with |T | = 25 , so dur = 24 (1h granularity). If Tgran = 4 , with |T | = 97 , then also dur = 24 (15 minutes granularity). 

Definition 3 (Sector Configuration) A sector at time t is a set of vertices (navpoints/airports). To keep the index set fixed across time, we represent each sector i by a distinguished sector identifier i ∈ V . We write S = V for the set of all identifiers. The sector identified by i at time t is the set sec (i, t ) ⊆ V .Formally, a sector configuration is a function sec : S × T → 2V such that for every t ∈ T : (i) {sec (i, t ) | i ∈ S, sec (i, t )̸ = ∅} forms a partition of V ; and (ii) every airport is a singleton sector. Conversely, sec −1 : V × T → S denotes the inverse assignment. The initial sector configuration at timestep ts = 0 is the atomic configuration, with atomic capacities cap (i, 0) = ci. Due to operational constraints, merging sectors does not lead to an increase in overall capacity. We model this by defining cap (i, t ) for a composite sector to be the max function. Example 3. We define two sector models on G from Example 1 and let Tgran =4 and |T | = 97 . Let the atomic capacities be cap (a0, 0) = cap (a1, 0) = 2 and 

cap (v0, 0) = cap (v1, 0) = cap (v2, 0) = 1. We define the static model as ∀t > 0 : 

sec (a0, t ) = {a0}, sec (a1, t ) = {a1}, sec (v0, t ) = {v0}, and sec (v1, t ) = {v1, v 2}.

Definition 5 (ATFCM Instance) An instance is I = ( G, T gran , T, sec , cap , A),comprising a navpoint graph G, time granularity Tgran , timesteps T , initial sector configurations sec , atomic capacities cap , and aircraft A. All identifiers are unique. 

Definition 6 (ATFCM Solution) A valid solution S = ( T, SEC , A) defines solution timesteps T ⊇ T , an updated DAC SEC , and adjusted aircraft A. We denote with F the set of adjusted flights stemming from A. We require: 

– Flight Adjustments: Flights f ∈ F: we measure arrival delay as df ; if f is delayed and/or rerouted, then rf = 1 (else 0). 

– Sector Constraints: SEC partitions navpoints over time (airports atomic). 

– Demand Routing: A flight f traversing navpoints va to vb in ∆t = tb − ta

occupies sec −1(va, t ) for t ∈ [ta, t a + ∆t/ 2] and sec −1(vb, t ) subsequently. We define over (f, i, t ) ∈ { 0, 1} to indicate if f occupies sector i at t.

– Capacity Constraints: Sector demand q(i, t ) = P

3.1 ASP Encoding: Definition of Instance 

Based on these definitions, we are ready to present the ASP instance and encoding. First, we are going to present the ASP instance with some preprocessing steps, followed by the encoding. In our prototype written in Python 3, the input as  

> 3

GitHub: https://github.com/alexl4123/ASPaeroFlow-Optimizer/tree/main/02_ASP 6 Beiser et. al. 

defined above is translated into an ASP instance, which we structure into three parts: navpoint graph instance , initial DAC instance , and filed flights instance .The navpoint graph instance defines the navpoint graph with the distinction between airports and navpoints. Additionally, we perform a preprocessing step which eases the computational load of ASP, by computing normalized distances w.r.t. Tgran based on aircraft velocities — so the distances are given in timesteps. 

Navpoint Graph Instance 

– arpt(<ID>) : Indicates that a vertex is an airport. 

– nvpt_e(<V_i>,<V_j>,<V_a>,< ∆t >) : For two connected vertices e = ( vi, v j ) ∈ E,we pre-compute the normalized travel duration ∆t for all normalized velocities of aircraft ua.

For the initial DAC instance we define atomic capacities and init configuration. 

Initial DAC Instance 

– atomic_s(<NVPT-ID>,<CAP>,<T>) : Initial capacities (assumed static). 

– nvpt_s(<NVPT-ID>,<SEC-ID>,<T=0 for FACTS>) : The initial sector configuration for time t = 0 .

The filed flights are given with the additional information about aircraft velocity and the mapping for multi-leg flights. 

Filed Flights Instance 

– acft(<ID>,<u_a>) : Aircraft ID and its velocity ua.

– acft_flt(<ACFT-ID>,<FLIGHT-ID>) : The mapping between aircraft and flights; needed among others for multi-leg flights. 

– nvpt_flt_pln(<FLT-ID>,<NVPT-ID>,<T>) : The filed flight plan as 3-tuples of flight ids, navpoint ids, and time steps.

3.2 ASP Encoding: Actions 

We continue to present the ASP encoding. The encoding is written in a way that enables us to perform an ablation study, which actions ( ground delaying ,

rerouting , or airspace restructuring ) are actually beneficial. For each action, we implement 3 versions, which gives us in total 27 possible ASP configurations for our ablation study. An ASP configuration is given by the 3-tuple (R, D, S ),with R ∈ { r, r p, ¬r}, D ∈ { d, d p, ¬d}, and S ∈ { s, s p, ¬s}. The Python wrapper translates a configuration (R, D, S ) to a set of facts, which provides instructions to the encoding. Here, we focus on the definitions of these facts and how this is translated in the encoding. We start with D, followed by R, and lastly S.Joint ATFCM via ASP 7

Ground Delaying ( D ∈ { d, d p, ¬d})

– d: Delaying along the entire considered time T

• ASP atom: reg_del 

– dp: Partial delaying (only max. <MAX-TIME> = 4 timesteps) 

• ASP atom: reg_restr_del 

• ASP atom: reg_dmd(<MAX-TIME>) 

– ¬d: Delaying is forbidden 

• ASP atom: -reg_del 

Ground delaying means that we guess the actual departure time depending on the atom ( d, d p, ¬d) present in the instance (Lines (1–4)). For dp, we restrict the guess to a pre-defined selection (Line (3)).                     

> 1p_dep_t ( ID , T ) ←flt_id ( ID ) , T=# min {T ’: nvpt_flt_pln ( ID ,_ ,T ’) }.
> 21{ a_dep_t ( ID , T ) : t ( T ) , T>= TP }1 ←p_dep_t ( ID , TP ) , reg_del .
> 31{ a_dep_t ( ID , T ) : t ( T ) ,T >= TP ,T <= TP + MD }1 ←p_dep_t ( ID , TP ) , reg_dmd ( MD ) , reg_rd .
> 4a_dep_t ( ID , TP ) ←p_dep_t ( ID , TP ) , - reg_del .

Rerouting ( R ∈ { r, r p, ¬r})

– r: Full rerouting is allowed (ASP finds paths). 

• ASP atom: reg_rer 

– rp: Partial rerouting; pick best of 4 diverse precomputed shortest paths (Jaccard similarity at most 60% ). Let <FLT> be the flight ID, <NVPT> be the navpoint at flight time <FLT-T> for a chosen path <PTH> .

• ASP atom: reg_restr_rer 

• ASP atom: reg_rrp(<FLT>,<NVPT>,<FLT-T>,<PTH>) 

– ¬r: Rerouting is forbidden. 

• ASP atom: -reg_rer 

We continue with R. First, we combine the ATFM side, whether a flight is rerouted or delayed, into one atom rer (Lines (1–2)). If r is enabled we guess a set of vertices (Line (4)), if rp is enabled then we guess one path of the provided ones (Lines (5)–(6)), and if rerouting is disabled ( ¬r) or rer is not set, we require that the flight follows the vertices of the filed flight (Lines (3) and (7)).

Airspace Restructuring ( S ∈ { s, s p, ¬s})

– s: Full airspace restructuring is allowed (no explicit operational constraints). 

• ASP atom: reg_dyn_sec_alloc .

– sp: Partial restructuring — 4 choices ( <D> ): (i) keep airspace structure, split into (ii) 2 or (iii) 4 connected sectors, or (iv) split into atomic structure. We enforce connected sectors in our partial restructuring to comply with typical operational needs of lowering the sector complexity . Let <S> be the original sector, <NVPT> be the navpoint, and <S1> the new sector. 8 Beiser et. al. 

• ASP atom: reg_rdsa 

• ASP atom: nvpt_restr_sec_alloc(<S>,<D>,<NVPT>,<S1>) 

– ¬s: Keep initial structure. 

• ASP atom: -reg_dyn_sec_alloc .

We continue with S. First, each airport sector is atomic (Line (1)). If s is enabled, then an arbitrary sectorization set is guessed (Line (2)), if sp is enabled, then one of four choices is picked (Lines (3)–(5)), and if ¬s is enabled, then we enforce the initial sectorization (Line (6)).

3.3 ASP Encoding: Constraints 

We require that the sectorization is a partition and adheres to our definition of a sectorization. Therefore, for each timestep, it must not be the case that a vertex is assigned to two sectors (Line (1)), a navpoint is assigned to an airport (Line (2)), an airport is assigned to a navpoint sector (Line (3)), or that a non-empty sector labeled by index i does not contain vertex i (Line (4)). 

1 ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC1 , T ) , nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC2 , T ) , SEC1 ̸ = SEC2 . 

2 ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC , T ) , not arpt ( NAV ) , arpt ( SEC ) . 

3 ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC , T ) , arpt ( NAV ) , not arpt ( SEC ) . 

4 ← nvpt_s ( NAV1 , SEC , T ) , NAV1 ̸ =SEC , not nvpt_s ( SEC , SEC , T ) . 

We further require that at any step the flight must not be operating before its planned departure time (Line (1)). Additionally, we require that for r the rerouting is an actual path, therefore the flight starts at the departure airport and lands at the destination airport (Lines (2–7)). A flight is finished if its destination is reached (Lines (8–9)). 

1 ← rer ( ID ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ , T ) , p_dep_t ( ID ,T ’) , T <T ’. 

2 planned_origin ( ID , X ) ← rer ( ID ) , p_dep_t ( ID , T ) , nvpt_flt_pln ( ID ,X , T ) . 

3 nvpt_flt ( ID ,X , T ) ← rer ( ID ) , a_dep_t ( ID , T ) , planned_origin ( ID , X ) . 

4 p_dest ( ID , X ) ← rer ( ID ) ,T =# max {T ’: nvpt_flt_pln ( ID ,_ ,T ’) } , nvpt_flt_pln ( ID ,X , T ) . 

5 a_arr_t ( ID , T ) ← flt_id ( ID ) , T =# max {T ’: nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ ,T ’) }. 

6 ← flt_id ( ID ) , not a_arr_t ( ID , _ ) . 

7 ← flt_id ( ID ) , p_dest ( ID , X ) , a_arr_t ( ID , T ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,X ’ , T ) , X̸ = X ’. 

8 a_dest_f_rch_t ( ID , T ) ← rer ( ID ) , p_dest ( ID , X ) , T =# min {T ’: nvpt_flt ( ID ,X ,T ’) }. 

9 ← rer ( ID ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ , T ) , a_dest_f_rch_t ( ID ,T ’) , T > T ’. 

We require that a flight is a valid trajectory, so is a path with valid timesteps (Lines (1–4)), that a flight is never twice at the same location (Line (5)), that a flight actually occurs (Lines (6–7)), and ensure that multi-leg flights occur in the right order (Line (8)). 

1 p_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ← nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ , T ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ , TT ) , T < TT . 

2 n_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ← p_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ,T < TTT , TTT < TT , p_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TTT ) . 

3 nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ← p_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) , not n_nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) . 

4 ← nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,X , T ) , nvpt_flt ( ID , XX , TT ) , acft_flt ( AID , ID ) , acft ( AID , S ) , D = TT -T , not nvpt_e (X , XX ,S , D ) . 

5 ← nvpt_flt ( ID ,X , T ) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,X ,T ’) , T̸ = T ’ , not arpt ( X ) . 

6 flightOccurs ( ID ) ← nvpt_flt ( ID ,_ , _ ) . 

7 ← flt_id ( ID ) , not flightOccurs ( ID ) . 

8 ← acft_flt ( AID , FID0 ) , acft_flt ( AID , FID1 ) , FID0 ̸=FID1 , p_arr_t ( FID0 , T0 ) , p_arr_t ( FID1 , T1 ) , T0 < T1 , a_arr_t ( FID0 , T0 ’) , a_dep_t ( FID1 , T1 ’) ,T0 ’ >= T1 ’. Joint ATFCM via ASP 9

3.4 ASP Encoding: Navpoint Flight Mapping 

Due to the joint modeling of DAC and flow optimization, we are left to map navpoint reach times to travel times. We approximate sector occupancy by assigning the first half of the flight between two navpoints to the first navpoint sector, and the second half to the second navpoint sector: let f ∈ F be a flight reaching navpoint va ∈ V at time ta and then flying to navpoint vb ∈ V , which it reaches at time tb. Assuming that ∆t = tb − ta, then f is in the timespan 

t ∈ [ta, t a + ⌊ ∆t 

2 ⌋] in sector sec −1(va, t ), and for t′ ∈ [ta + ⌊ ∆t 

2 ⌋ + 1 , t b] in sector 

sec −1(vb, t ′). We encode this in Lines (1–5), while we forbid “holes” (Line (6)). 

1 flight ( ID ,S , T ) ← nvpt_flt ( ID ,X , T ) , nvpt_s (X ,S , T ) . 

2 flightT ( ID ,T ,T ’ ,0) ← nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ,D = TT -T , t (T ’) ,T ’ >T ,T ’ <= T + D /2. 

3 flightT ( ID , TT ,T ’ ,1) ← nvpt_seq ( ID ,T , TT ) ,D = TT -T , t (T ’) ,T ’ < TT ,T ’ > T + D /2. 

4 flight ( ID ,S ,T ’) ← flightT ( ID ,T ,T ’ ,0) , nvpt_flt ( ID ,X , T ) , nvpt_s (X ,S ,T ’) . 

5 flight ( ID ,S ,T ’) ← flightT ( ID , TT ,T ’ ,1) , nvpt_flt ( ID , XX , TT ) , nvpt_s ( XX ,S ,T ’) . 

6 ← t ( T ) , a_dep_t ( ID ,T ’) , T > T ’ , a_arr_t ( ID ,T ’ ’) , T < T ’ ’ , not flight ( ID ,_ , T ) . 

3.5 ASP Encoding: Optimization 

We map the optimization problem of Definition 7 into a multi-objective lexi-cographic optimization ASP program. Regarding specifics, we treat safety as a soft constraint in order to track its computational results in the experiment section. Still, only 0-overload solutions are valid solutions. Overall, we have then six optimization criteria: the total number of overloads followed by the criteria from Definition 7. 

1 sec_len ( SEC ,T , CL ) ← t ( T ) , nvpt ( SEC ) , CL =# count { NAV : nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC , T ) }. 

2 sec ( SEC ,T ,0) ← sec_len ( SEC ,T , CAP_LEN ) , CAP_LEN = 0. 

3 sec ( SEC ,T , C ) ← t ( T ) , nvpt ( SEC ) , C = # max {C ’ , NAV : nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC , T ) , atomic_s ( NAV ,C ’ , T ) } , sec_len ( SEC ,T , CAP_LEN ) , CAP_LEN > 0. 

4 overload (X ,T , LOAD - C ) ← sec (X ,T , C ) , # count { ID : flight ( ID ,X , T ) }= LOAD , LOAD > C . 

5⇝ overload (X ,T , OVER ) . [ OVER@10 ,X , T ] % PRIMARY WEAK CONSTRAINT 

We use efficiency objectives as our secondary objectives, which follows com-mon practice in the literature [ 3]. However, we expand the current practice by incorporating airspace efficiency metrics, which we partially take from the DAC literature. Generally speaking, DAC wants to have as few sectors as possible with as few changes as possible, while additionally minimizing sector complexity. As proxies for this, people may use minimization of excess capacity [ 17 ], or explicitly minimizing for controller communication overload [ 18 ]. We minimize the number of sector changes, while considering airspace complexity with our partial sectorization approach ( sp). 

1 p_arr_t ( ID , T ) ← flt_id ( ID ) , T = # max {T ’: nvpt_flt_pln ( ID ,_ ,T ’) }. 

2 arrival_delay ( ID ,Y - T ) ← p_arr_t ( ID , T ) , a_arr_t ( ID , Y ) . 

3 sector_number (T , NUM ) ← t ( T ) , NUM = # count { SEC : sec_len ( SEC ,T , C ) , C > 0}. 

4 nvpt_ch ( NAV ,T ,1) ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC0 , T ) , nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC1 , T +1) , SEC0 ̸=SEC1 . 

5 nvpt_ch ( NAV ,T ,0) ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC0 , T ) , nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC0 , T +1) . 

6 sector_diff (T , DIFF ) ← t ( T ) , DIFF = # sum {V , NAV : nvpt_ch ( NAV ,T , V ) }. 

7⇝ arrival_delay ( ID , DIFF ) . [ DIFF@9 , ID ] % SECONDARY WEAK CONSTRAINT 

8⇝ sector_number (T , NUM ) . [ NUM@8 , T ] % TERTIARY WEAK CONSTRAINT 

9⇝ sector_diff (T , DIFF ) . [ DIFF@7 , T ] % OPTIMIZE FOR LEAST SECTOR CHANGES 

Lastly, we optimize for fairness/stability to the original plan, where we follow the general plan of optimizing for flow first and then airspace. 

1 reconfig ( NAV , T ) ← nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC ,0) , nvpt_s ( NAV , SEC2 , T ) , SEC ̸ = SEC2 , T > 0. 

2⇝ rer ( ID ) . [1 @6 , ID ] % OPTIMIZE FOR LEAST REROUTES / DELAYS 

3⇝ reconfig ( ID , T ) . [1 @5 , ID , T ] % OPTIMIZE FOR LEAST RECONFIGS 10 Beiser et. al.

Solving this instance leads to the following results: 0 overload, 0 arrival delay, 294 

number of sectors, 3 sector-diffs, 1 rerouting, and 94 reconfigurations. The solution of the sectorization is: ∀t > 0: sec (a0, t ) = {a0} and sec (a1, t ) = {a1}. For t ≤ 3

and t ≥ 6 it is: sec (v1, t ) = {v0, v 1, v 2}, whereas for t ∈ { 4, 5} it is: sec (v0, t ) = 

{v0} and sec (v1, t ) = {v1, v 2}. Further, the optimized flight trajectories are: Fa =

{(0 , (( a0, 1) , (v1, 4) , (a1, 7))) , (1 , (( a1, 1) , (v0, 5) , (a0, 9))) }. The trajectory-sector mappings are: f0 : ( a0, 1) , (a0, 2) , (v1, 3) , (v1, 4) , (v1, 5) , (a1, 6) , (a1, 7) ; f1 : ( a1, 1) ,

(a1, 2) , (a1, 3) , (v0, 4) , (v0, 5) , (v1, 6) , (v1, 7) , (a0, 8) , (a0, 9) . The (total) number of sectors is also optimal: each airport must be a singleton sector, therefore their contribution is 96 · 2. The en-route sector contribution is 96 · 1 for v1 and 2 · 1

for v0 (for t ∈ { 4, 5}). As the lowest possible number of en-route total sectors is 96 , any lower number than 98 = 96 + 2 would result in merging v1 and v0,which would lead to an overload. This results in a (total) number of sectors of 

290 (for t > 0), which in addition with the initial configuration with 4 sectors for 

t = 0 yields in 294 . The solution changes the assignment of navpoint v0 three times: once between t = 0 and t = 1 , between t = 3 and t = 4 and between t = 5 

and t = 6 , leading to sector diff of 3. Further, flight 1 is rerouted from flying over navpoint v2 to flying via v0 and finally, 22 reconfigurations results from reconfigurations of v0 in 94 timesteps ( t̸ ∈ { 0, 4, 5}), yielding 97 − 3 = 94 .

3.7 Correctness 

We briefly argue for correctness for ASP (r, d, s ). Let I be an arbitrary instance; let Π be the encoding, then Π′ is the encoding without soft constraints, but with c1 := {← overload (X, T, OV ER )}. Let AS be the answersets of Π′. For any flight f ∈ F, let taf be its actual arrival time. Let c(I) be instance converted to ASP without max _t(t) (Section 3.1); for answersets AS ∈ AS let c−1(AS )

be the converted corresponding solution S where T is chosen to be max f ∈F taf .Let S=t be the set of valid solutions for I s.t. ∀S ∈ S =t : max {T} = t, where we require max f ∈F{taf } = t; further S≤t = S 

> t′∈{ 0,...,t }

S=t′ .

## 4 Pipeline Integration 

In Figure 2 we show the integration of the encoding into the overall pipeline. Based on actual data, we developed an open source data generator, which enables us to sample data from different geographic regions and with an arbitrary number of flights. This benchmark data is then pre-processed in Python, which again starts the ASP solving loop: we start with a fixed maximum time ( max_t(t) ), which is iteratively expanded until a solution with 0 overload is found 4.

4.1 An Open Source Data Generator 

Data in ATM research is often closed source, due to privacy concerns or the fear of competitive disadvantage. Although these concerns are understandable from an economic perspective, they are disadvantageous for science — which leads to two common issues: (a) published research cannot be reproduced, thereby manifesting itself in a so-called reproducibility crisis; (b) researchers relying on open data are sometimes forced to use datasets that are ill-suited to the task. In order to circumvent this problem, movements have been initiated, such as the formation of the Open Science Alliance for ATM research [ 4]. However, open data for ATFCM research is still scarce. This paper takes a pro-open-data stance, by creating a data generator built from publicly available open data. Our data generator is available on GitHub 5 and we published the dataset used in the experiments on Zenodo 6. The Data Generator executes three sequential internal phases to construct problem instances: 

– Graph Generation: Constructs a configurable grid-graph of navpoints augmented with real-world airport coordinates using the public OurAirports 

dataset. 

– Flight Pair Generation: Employs a stochastic point process [ 15 ] to sample flight operations. The underlying Poisson model is parameterized by historical departure and arrival intensities derived from the OpenSky Network COVID-19 surveillance dataset [22]. 

– Trajectory & Sector Generation: Computes baseline trajectories via 

shortest-path routing for origin-destination pairs. Initial airspace configu-rations are partitioned using a Breadth-First Search (BFS) algorithm to guarantee sectors are connected or geometrically convex.

## 5 Experiments 

We conduct experiments on 5 graph scenarios, as shown in Table 1. Thereby, we enable a scaling study from 16 to 117 vertices. Further, we range flights from 10 to 100, with steps of 10 . For each such combination of geographic and flight scenario, we generated 4 instances with different seeds. The size of these instances was chosen to be able to conduct experiments with exact solvers. Industry-sized instances largely depend upon the selected area of investigation and range from several hundreds of flights and sectors up to 30 , 000 flights, ≈ 10 3 sectors, and 

≈ 10 5 navpoints for European airspace. However, SOTA research using exact MIP models considers smaller instances which go up to approximately ≈ 3, 000 

flights with bounded choices, while considering static sector configurations [ 13 ]. By enabling more degrees of freedom and dynamic alternative rerouting/delaying choices, we cannot expect to solve instances with thousands of flights. 

Variants . We consider 27 variants of the ASP encoding, where each variant is defined by the previously defined 3-tuple (R, D, S ). Our model implements a heuristic proxy for the CASA algorithm, iteratively assigning ground delays to aircraft to resolve sector capacity constraints. We denote the baseline solution as INITIAL, which are the values of the instance without any actions taken. Further, we simulate a SOTA dynamic MIP algorithm, that acts on delaying and rerouting aircraft, with bounded delay and reroute choices. 

Technical Setup . Experiments were conducted on a cluster with 2x Intel Xeon Silver 4314 CPUs, 512GB RAM, and Ubuntu 22.04 (Kernel 5.15.0-131-generic). The compute budget is limited by a TIMEOUT of 1800 s and a MEMOUT of 

35 GB. We use Python 3.13.7 with Clingo (5.8.0) API, which preprocesses and converts the input to an ASP instance. For MIP we use Gurobi (build v13.0.0rc1). 

Results and Discussion . We evaluate the suitability of ASP for ATFCM (Tables 2 and 3; Figures 3 and 4). 

– Lexicographic Performance: CASA achieves strict feasibility ( 0 overload) but incurs massive arrival delay ( 2353 .12 h). In contrast, ASP (rp, d, s p)provides a superior operational trade-off, achieving an overload of 23 .27 with 

211 .44 h delay. Arguably, ASP (rp, d p, s p) offers an even stronger operational balance; while its overload is slightly higher ( 29 .55 ), its arrival delay ( 82 .01 

h) is strictly lower than the MIP baseline ( 48 .67 overload, 128 .52 h delay). All highlighted variants significantly outperform the unmodified Initial state (92 .39 overload). Joint ATFCM via ASP 13

± shows the standard error of the mean. 

– Computational Bottlenecks: For heuristically bounded variants, solving time dominates the computational effort, leading to TIMEOUTs on larger instances. However, enabling unrestricted rerouting ( r) triggers a grounding bottleneck. This manifests in drastically larger grounding times and a severe memory explosion (averaging 5867 .73 MB and 50 .09 s grounding time) com-pared to partial ( rp, 439 .13 MB and 1.93 s grounding time) or no rerouting (¬r, 406 .81 MB and 1.67 s grounding time). These results demonstrate that shifting spatial routing and sectorization from the solver to a heuristic pre-processor is critical to bypass memory and time bottlenecks. We attribute the performance discrepancy between our exact ASP 

models and reported MIP literature to our looser model definitions and the treatment of rerouting alternatives as dynamic rather than static data. 

TTF/TTB Analysis . Figure 4 visualizes the search dynamics by plotting Time to First Solution (TTF) against Time to Best Solution (TTB). We quantify the optimization phase using the median time gap ∆t = TTB − TTF and the median percentage reduction in initial overload om.

– Search Space Thrashing: The unbounded variant ASP (r, d, s ) struggles to find initial feasibility and frequently terminates without improving the initial bound (clustering on the diagonal). It yields the lowest optimization impact ( ∆t = 878 .74 s, om = 34 .10% ). 

– Fast Feasibility: Tightly constrained variants rapidly establish initial upper bounds, efficiently utilizing the remaining budget to traverse the search space 14 Beiser et. al. 16 32 48 57 117

Fig. 3: Scaling results of CASA (top left), MIP (top right), ASP ( rp, d, s p) (bottom left), and ASP ( rp, d p, s p) (bottom right). X-axis shows size of the considered geographical instance ( |V |) and Y-axis shows number of flights. Each data point has three instances, where the hatching is shown iff all seeds achieve 0 overload. (∆t > 1500 s, om > 76% ). Visual clustering differentiates their behavior: ASP 

(rp, d p, s p) strictly clusters in the top-left (extremely fast TTF, sustained late TTB), whereas ASP (¬r, d, ¬s) clusters primarily in the center-top and left-mid, indicating earlier search space exhaustion. 

– Adequate Convergence: The globally best-performing variant, ASP (rp, d, s p), is bounded in the scatter plot by the extremes of the other methods. It requires slightly more time to establish initial feasibility than fully constrained models but sustains a robust optimization phase ( ∆t = 1644 .49 s, om = 74 .88% ). 

Open Challenge: Frequent timeouts of ASP shows that exact solvers cannot yet prove optimality for large instances within operational time limits. 

## 6 Conclusion 

We present a unified and extensible formalization of Air Traffic Flow and Capacity Management (ATFCM) that integrates flow management decisions and dynamic airspace configuration (DAC). Unlike existing formulations that treat these components in isolation or assume pre-defined sector changes, our model captures delaying, rerouting, and DAC within a single navpoint-graph-based framework. We develop an ASP encoding to investigate the feasibility of ASP in the ATFCM domain. Our ASP encoding is able to freely switch between full/partial/disabled actions, which enable the conduction of an ablation study, where we further compare our encoding to a simulation of the practical CASA algorithm and a simulation of a SOTA MIP model. Our results indicate that ASP with partial sectorization is able to achieve strong results, especially for smaller instance sizes. Joint ATFCM via ASP 15 10 1 10 0 10 1 10 2 10 3        

> Time to First Solution (TTF) [s]
> 10 1
> 10 0

> Variant

> Scenario
> East Asia (3x3)

> USA East Coast (7x7)
> Major Europe (10x10)

Fig. 4: Time to First Solution [TTF] vs. Time to Best Solution (TTB) scatter plot comparing four selected ASP variants. When the instance sizes grow larger, the computational budget does not suffice for ASP to finish the computation, which leads to the result that the CASA heuristics is the only tested variant able to solve all benchmarked instances to 0-overload — however, with the downside of having low efficiency (high delay), in comparison to ASP’s solutions. Our ablation study of the ASP variants indicates, that the size of the search space should be “just big enough”, as restrictions of the search space to a fixed number of alternatives lead to the best results. Several directions are promising for future work. First, the lack of theoretical analysis is a hindrance for abstract modelling, where we plan to analyze which modelling choices drive computational hardness. Second, we will investigate the combination of ASP into a hybrid heuristic solver for larger instances. Third, due to the inherent safety criticality of the domain, we will enable operational explainability by generating human-interpretable justifications for delays, reroutes, and reconfigurations.

## B Additional Notes on ATM 

Air Traffic Management (ATM). ATM is a broad field which aims at ensuring safe and efficient air traffic at all times. In Europe, the primary entitiy responsible for ATM is EUROCONTROL, while in the USA it is the Federal Aviation Administration (FAA). We introduce ATFCM along a timeline, which is anchored around a day of operation (DOP). At the DOP, ATCs need to separate aircraft – which is called the tactical phase. In en-route airspace (cruise phase of a flight) any two aircraft must be separated by at least 5 nm horizontally, or 1000 f t 

vertically [ 9,20 ]. ATCs operate in a sector , which is a defined volume of airspace. Due to the human nature, ATCs can only effectively handle a certain number of flights in a given time period, which is the primary reason why sectors have 

capacity constraints. The exact capacity value is dependent on various factors, ranging from the competence of the ATC staff, over managerial and technical matters such as ATC shift planning, used automation aids or sector geography, to temporary effects, such as thunderstorms or worker union strikes [ 9]. Evidently, when demand - the number of aircraft that want to fly through a sector - exceeds capacity, this puts additional workload on ATCs and therefore increases the likelihood of human error, with potential catastrophic consequences. Therefore, it is required that demand remains below capacity at all times. On the other hand, the responsibility of the strategic level is to ensure that sectors are not overloaded - which is also called Air Traffic Flow (and Capacity) Management (ATFCM). Generally, airlines file flights months in advance. Filing a flight effectively means to register a flight that will in the future start at a departure airport and take a certain route to fly to its destination. This gives air navigation service providers (ANSPs) and EUROCONTROL time to react to potential imbalances. For example, ANSPs may conduct managerial decisions, like sending teams of ATCs to another facility, where more demand is expected, or 18 Beiser et. al. 

on the very far end of the spectrum training more ATC staff. Closer to the DOP, the so-called network manager of EUROCONTROL is responsible for mitigating remaining imbalances by taking regulations to either reduce demand or increase capacity. Measures for reducing demand include delaying or rerouting aircraft. Effective capacity may be increased by DAC [ 18 , 10 ], which either restructures sectors to enhance flow, or opens sectors exactly at positions where they are needed the most. 

## C Detailed Model Definition 

Definition 8 (Navpoint Graph) Detailed definition of Definition 1. The nav-point graph G = ( V, E ) is undirected and labeled. We require that V = N≤| V |, i.e., each v is an integer and and V has no holes. Each v ∈ V is either a navpoint or an airport, which we test with v ∈ en-route (G) and v ∈ airport (G). Distances between two navpoints are defined as the function d : V 2 → Q, which we assume to be computed in O(1) .

Definition 9 (Time model, extends Definition 2) Time is discretized into timesteps ti ∈ T , with Tgran timesteps per hour s.t. Tgran divides 60 such that the timestep length is defined in minutes by Ts = 60  

> Tgran

. T is the set of timesteps, which for a dur ∈ N is given by T = {t | t ∈ N, 1 ≤ t ≤ dur · Tgran } ∪ { 0}.

Definition 10 (Sector Model, extends Definition 3) A sector at time t is a set of vertices (navpoints/airports). To keep the index set fixed across time, we represent each sector by a distinguished sector identifier i ∈ V . We write 

S = V for the set of all identifiers. The sector identified by i at time t is the set sec (i, t ) ⊆ V . Formally, a sector configuration is a function sec : S × T → 2V

such that for every t ∈ T : (i) {sec (i, t ) | i ∈ S, sec (i, t )̸ = ∅} forms a partition of 

V ; and (ii) every airport is a singleton sector. More precisely, let t ∈ T , then for any i, j ∈ S s.t. i̸ = j it must hold sec (i, t ) ∩ sec (j, t ) = ∅ and S 

> i∈S

sec (i, t ) = V .Conversely, sec −1 : V × T → S denotes the inverse assignment. The initial sector configuration at timestep ts = 0 is the atomic configuration, with atomic capacities cap (i, 0) = ci.

Definition 11 (Composite Sector Model) The capacity of composite sec-tors for t > 0 is defined by cap ′ : S × T → N, where we let for i ∈ S:cap ′(i, t ) = max v∈SEC (i,t ){cap (v, 0) }. The capacity cap ′ is measured in 1-hour timeframes and distributed in the following way. For each 1-hour timeframe, there are Tgran timesteps, each with capacitiy cap (i, t ). ## E Dynamic MIP Model 

We simulate the SOTA MIP model with the following dynamic MIP model. Dynamic in the sense that the ground delay and reroute alternatives are generated by the Python wrapper. Otherwise, we restrict the MIP model to a SOTA model in the sense that it only optimizes routes and delay, but not DAC. Let F be the set of flights, where f ∈ F has a departure ( source f ) and an arrival ( destination f ) airport, with associated departure time tstart and expected arrival time ˆtdest . Let G be the navpoint graph. The set of paths P is the set of simple paths between any two vertices in the navpoint graWe create for each jobph G. Then let Pf be the set of paths restricted to f: Pf = {p | p =

⟨source f , . . . , destination f ⟩ ∈ P }.Further, let T be the set of times in the whole application, whereas, T f,p is the set of times that an airplane needs to travel from source f to destination f . Given a flight f ∈ F , a path p ∈ Pf , and a specific time during the flight t ∈ T f,p , we can compute the estimated current sector-position of the flight j = Sf,p t . Lastly, let S be the set of sectors. We interpret the sectors as the Voronoi diagram of the navpoints, where each sector may have a set of corresponding navpoints. The location of the flight along a path is computed according to the Voronoi interpretation. A sector j ∈ S has a capacity per time unit Cj (t). Example: When a flight f starts at t = 0 at navpoint a (sector A) and travels to navpoint b

(sector B) - and the duration of the flight takes ∆t = 3 units of time, then flight 

f is in sector A during time units {0, 1}, and in sector B during time units {2, 3}.24 Beiser et. al.

Further, let isFirst (j, t, f, p ) evaluate to true iff ∀t′ ∈ T f,p : t′ < t it holds that i = Sf,p t′ , where i̸ = j. Conversely, isLast (j, t, f, p ) evaluates to true iff 

∀t′ ∈ T f,p : t′ > t it holds that i = Sf,p t′ , where i̸ j.We treat capacity violations as a hard constraint and optimize for efficiency (to be more in line with SOTA work). In the future, we plan to adapt this SOAT simulation model to a new model in our formalism, by incorporating DAC optimization and lexicographic optimization. 

Description of Equations . We define variables for flightss, delays, and paths in Equation (7). We require that exactly one departing flight is chosen (Equa-tions (5) and (6)). If a path is chosen it must be kept throughout the flight (Equation (2)) and there must not be more than one path at any other node (Equation (3)). Similarly, at any node along the path there must only be one path active (Equation (4)). At any given timestep, a sector must not be overloaden (Equation (9)) and for an airplane with multiple flights per day, it must not be that the departure time tstart of the next flight is before the landing time of the previous flight tdest (Equation (8)). One may switch Equation (9) to Equation (10) to switch demand measurement from number of airplanes in a sector, to airplanes entering a sector.

## F Additional Details on Experiments 

We show additional details of the experiments. In Tables 4 and 5 we show the tournament wins and number of solved instances, overall and only for the ASP variants, respectively. In Tables 6–7 we show the results when structured along the geographic and flight scenarios. In Figures 6–10 we show averaged metrics for all variants, geographic scenarios, and flight sizes, for the ASP variants as heat maps. Finally, in Table 8 we show the detailed results for all variants, for all instances. 

## G Omitted Proofs 

Lemma 1 ( ⋆) Solution Space Equivalence . For any t ≥ 24 · Tgran , the sets 

SAS := {c−1(AS ) | AS ∈ AS (Π′ ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)})} and S≤t match. Proof. SAS ⊆ S ≤t: let s = ( T, SEC , A) ∈ S AS be an arbitrary converted answerset. It is also a solution for s ∈ S ≤t: due to s = c−1(AS ), AS must be an answerset of Π′ ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)}. SEC is a partition of navpoints over time and A is are valid flights due to Section 3.3. Further, max {T} = t′, where t′ ≤ t.Lastly, Section 3.4 ensures that the trajectories are correctly mapped to sector occupancies and with c1 no overloads occur in the answerset AS .

S≤t ⊆ S AS : let s = ( T, SEC , A) ∈ S ≤t be an arbitrary valid solution. Then also s ∈ S AS : first, due to max _t(t) it must hold for any answerset of 

Π′ ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)} that max f ∈F taf ≤ t. Further, due to having ASP (r, d, s )

and Section 3.2, the considerd flights in the solver are all departure times and trajectories (under the time limit t), combined with all possible sector assignments; both are restricted to the feasibility constraints of Definition 6. Therefore also 

s ∈ S AS .

Theorem 1 (⋆). Solution Existence. Let I be an instance and t ≥ 24 · Tgran .

Π ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)} has a 0-overload answerset iff there exists a solution 

S ∈ S ≤t for the optimization problem (Definition 7). Proof. Proof (Idea): By Lemma 1 we know that the answersets of the encoding corresponds to all valid solutions within the same timeframe; we are left to show that this translates to the optimization problem. In contrast to Π′, Π does not have a hard constraint on the overload but additional soft constraints. However, requiring a 0-overload answerset Π′ corresponds to c1 being active. The remaining soft constraints of Π do not shrink the solution space, they only define an order upon them, which leads the statement to hold. 

Theorem 2 (⋆). Optimality. Let I be an arbitrary instance, let t ≥ 24 · Tgran ,and let one of its best solutions Sopt be found within max {T} = t : Sopt ∈ S ≤t.Then Π ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)} is optimal. Proof. Towards a contradiction assume the statement does not hold, so for the found optimal answerset {AS } = AS (Π ∪ c(I) ∪ { max _t(t)}) it holds Joint ATFCM via ASP 29 

Fig. 5: Navpoint counter example graph G = ( V, E ) for Observation 1. 

Sopt ≺ c−1(AS ). First, observe that by Lemma 1 it holds Sopt ∈ S AS for 

Π′. Therefore, the soft constraints in Π have to rule out Sopt . However, they define a lexicographic optimization (Section 3.5), equivalent to the optimization Definition 7. The highest priority has reducing overloads with a priority of 

10 , followed by Kd with a priority of 9, K#s with a priority of 8, Kds with a priority of 7, Kr with a priority of 6, and Kc with a priority of 5. But therefore the answerset corresponding to Sopt is lexicographically better than AS and is therefore preferred, which contradicts our assumption. Note that the pipeline implementation does not guarantee to find an optimal solution, it still guarantees to find a solution. To ensure termination, we break the loop if t > |T | + 12 · | T | · | F | · (|F | + 1) , as further search is not necessary to find a (optimal) solution. See also Theorem 3, Observations 1 and 2, and Corollaries 2 and 1. These results hold for df = max {taf − tef , 0}, where taf is the actual and tef

expected arrival time. This holds for all generated instance implicitly, one can explicitly enforce it via:

Corollary 1 The prototype (pipeline) implementation is not optimal. Proof. Directly follows from Observation 1. 

Observation 2 The delay of sequential flight scheduling (max one flight air-borne) is upper bounded by Kseq d ≤ 12 · | F | · (|F | + 1) · | T |.

Theorem 3. Let I be an instance, where Sf eas is an arbitrary valid solution thereof with delay Kf eas d . There exists an optimal solution Sopt such that: 

max {Topt } ≤ max   

> f∈F

{tef } + Kf eas d

Proof. Since Sopt optimizes arrival delay as its highest priority, its total delay must satisfy Kopt d ≤ Kf eas d . Since individual delays are non-negative ( df ≥ 0), no single flight can absorb a delay greater than the total delay: ∀f ∈ F : df ≤ Kopt d ≤ Kf eas d .The arrival time of any flight f in the optimal solution is taf = tef + df . Note 

|T | − 1 = max {T }. Therefore, max {Topt } = max {max f {tef + df } ∪ {| T | − 1}} ≤ 

max f ∈F{tef } + Kf eas d .

Corollary 2 Let I be an instance, where Sseq is a valid solution where flights are sequentially scheduled. There exists an optimal solution Sopt such that: 

max {Topt } ≤ | T | + 12 · | F | · (|F | + 1) · | T |

Proof. By Theorem 3 we know max {Topt } ≤ max f ∈F{tef } + Kseq d and by Obser-vation 2 Kseq d = 12 · | F | · (|F | + 1) · | T |. Therefore max {Topt } ≤ max f ∈F{tef } + 

> 12

· | F | · (|F | + 1) · | T |. Further, we know max f ∈F{tef } ≤ | T |, and therefore 

max {Topt } ≤ | T | + 12 · | F | · (|F | + 1) · | T |.

## H Limitations 

“All models are wrong but some are useful”, is a phrase which captures the core of our limitations well. Whether our model will be useful will be judged by time, where we think that it can be a cornerstone of pushing research in ATFCM to more comparable and grounded work. Therefore, our mathematical formal model applies abstractions to the safety-critical socio-technical ATFCM task. For example, we need to discretize time for our time model and space for our navpoint graph, which may induce compounding errors. Further, our current version does not model several aspects, which however, are required for operational use — where we argue that they can be integrated in future work, such as: (a) variable speeds of aircraft, (b) uncertainty and stochasticity, for example due to weather, or (c) modelling passengers/airlines and fairness, . . . Joint ATFCM via ASP 31 

## I Statement on the use of AI 

As we are non-native English speakers, we improved the readability of the paper by using generative AI for spelling, grammar, and wording suggestions. AI was used as an assistant during the development phase of the prototype including setting up and running the experiments on the cluster. Further, AI was partially used to generate Python scripts for plotting the figures (except schematics, which we made ourselves entirely) and generating the L ATEXtables. We used variants of ChatGPT-5 and Gemini 3.1 Pro.
